**Explanation:** Under the *Dingwall* approach, imminence does not require physical proximity of the threatening party. A pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger, making a jury question appropriate under these modified facts. Option (b) falsely claims *Dingwall* applies only when the defendant is physically confined; *Dingwall* explicitly held physical presence is not required. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely; it relaxes it but does not eliminate it. Option (d) falsely limits the *Dingwall* reasoning to intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome; while *Dingwall* involved a battering relationship, its logic regarding how sustained threats satisfy imminence applies to duress generally and is not categorically restricted to IPV. Option (e) falsely states expert testimony is a mandatory procedural precondition; *Dingwall* imposes no such requirement.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students apply the strict physical proximity test even in circuits that have relaxed it, or limit Dingwall to IPV cases

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- audit: MUST FIX
Check 1: finding - The marked answer (a) misapplies the doctrine. Dingwall does not establish a blanket rule relaxing imminence for any "continuous, sustained" threats (which would allow the exception to swallow the standard duress rule for gang/mafia cases). Its holding specifically applies to victims of "battering and its effects" and relies on the unique constraints of coercive control.
Check 2: finding - Option (d) is a highly defensible distractor and arguably the legally correct answer. A prepared student would recognize that courts ring-fence the Dingwall/BWS exception to cases involving battering or coercive control, meaning it would not extend to a generalized dispute between "former associates."
Check 3: finding - The explanation claims Dingwall's logic "applies to duress generally and is not categorically restricted to IPV." This misrepresents the case, which explicitly tailored its holding to "a defendant who presents evidence of being a victim of battering and its effects."
Check 4: pass
Check 5: pass
Check 6: pass (The stem commendably avoids graphic DV facts by using former associates to test the boundary of the doctrine; referencing IPV/BWS in the options is appropriate given the `coercive-control-vs-bws` map tag).
Check 7: pass
Recommended fix: Change the correct answer to (d) and update the explanation to reflect that Dingwall’s relaxation of imminence is grounded in the specific psychology of battering/coercive control, meaning a defendant cannot invoke it for generalized "former associate" threats. Alternatively, rewrite the stem to involve a coercive control dynamic (without graphic violence) if you want (a) to be correct.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Dingwall reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. Would the duress analysis change? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because under the Dingwall approach, a pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the threatening party is not physically present at the time of the crime, and the combination of months of threats, Nietzsche's history of carrying out threats, and Holmes's reasonable belief that police cannot help would support submitting the duress claim to a jury. <!-- correct -->
(b) No, because the Dingwall reasoning relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained by the threatening party at the time of the crime, and Holmes was free to move about and had access to means of self-help.
(c) Yes, because the Dingwall approach eliminates the imminence requirement entirely and replaces it with a credibility inquiry, so any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote in time -- satisfies the duress defense if the threatening party has a track record of violence.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception to strict imminence is explicitly limited to cases involving intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome, and the dispute between Holmes and his former associate Nietzsche falls outside that limitation.
(e) Yes, but only if Holmes can present expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats, because the Dingwall approach requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition to submitting the duress claim to the jury.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Under the *Dingwall* approach, imminence does not require physical proximity of the threatening party. A pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger, making a jury question appropriate under these modified facts. Option (b) falsely claims *Dingwall* applies only when the defendant is physically confined; *Dingwall* explicitly held physical presence is not required. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely; it relaxes it but does not eliminate it. Option (d) falsely limits the *Dingwall* reasoning to intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome; while *Dingwall* involved a battering relationship, its logic regarding how sustained threats satisfy imminence applies to duress generally and is not categorically restricted to IPV. Option (e) falsely states expert testimony is a mandatory procedural precondition; *Dingwall* imposes no such requirement.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students apply the strict physical proximity test even in circuits that have relaxed it, or limit Dingwall to IPV cases

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- edge-case-audit: MUST FIX
1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: MUST FIX. At common law, the duress defense strictly requires that the coercer command the defendant to commit the *specific crime* charged. In Fact 4, Nietzsche merely demands money ("bring me $5,000 by Friday") and does not command Holmes to steal it from the shelter. Because Holmes independently chose theft as the means to satisfy the extortion demand, the duress defense fails as a matter of law. Therefore, Option (a) is incorrect when it states that relaxing the imminence requirement alone "would support submitting the duress claim to a jury"—a judge would still bar the defense entirely due to the lack of a specific criminal command.
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: Pass.
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: Pass.
Recommended fix: Modify Q5's prompt to state: "Assume further that Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money from the shelter, had been threatening..." OR edit Fact 4 directly so Nietzsche says: "If you don't steal $5,000 from that shelter and bring it to me..."
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Dingwall reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. Would the duress analysis change? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because under the Dingwall approach, a pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the threatening party is not physically present at the time of the crime, and the combination of months of threats, Nietzsche's history of carrying out threats, and Holmes's reasonable belief that police cannot help would support submitting the duress claim to a jury. <!-- correct -->
(b) No, because the Dingwall reasoning relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained by the threatening party at the time of the crime, and Holmes was free to move about and had access to means of self-help.
(c) Yes, because the Dingwall approach eliminates the imminence requirement entirely and replaces it with a credibility inquiry, so any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote in time -- satisfies the duress defense if the threatening party has a track record of violence.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception to strict imminence is explicitly limited to cases involving intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome, and the dispute between Holmes and his former associate Nietzsche falls outside that limitation.
(e) Yes, but only if Holmes can present expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats, because the Dingwall approach requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition to submitting the duress claim to the jury.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Under the *Dingwall* approach, imminence does not require physical proximity of the threatening party. A pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger, making a jury question appropriate under these modified facts. Option (b) falsely claims *Dingwall* applies only when the defendant is physically confined; *Dingwall* explicitly held physical presence is not required. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely; it relaxes it but does not eliminate it. Option (d) falsely limits the *Dingwall* reasoning to intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome; while *Dingwall* involved a battering relationship, its logic regarding how sustained threats satisfy imminence applies to duress generally and is not categorically restricted to IPV. Option (e) falsely states expert testimony is a mandatory procedural precondition; *Dingwall* imposes no such requirement.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students apply the strict physical proximity test even in circuits that have relaxed it, or limit Dingwall to IPV cases

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the Dingwall reasoning (United States v. Dingwall), the traditional requirement of immediate, physical proximity of the threatener is relaxed. Where a defendant can show a history of sustained and unpredictable threats, the "imminence" of the danger becomes a question of the defendant's reasonable perception of constant peril. These modified facts—months of threats, a track record of Nietzsche following through on violence, and the absence of police protection—fit the Dingwall paradigm perfectly. This allows the duress defense to reach the jury even if the threatener is not "at the elbow" of the defendant.

(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that the logic of Dingwall is rooted in the "inescapability" of the defendant's situation. If Holmes was "free to move about" and not physically confined (unlike a hostage or a trapped domestic partner), he fails the threshold for a relaxed imminence standard. Under this view, the Dingwall exception is reserved for situations where physical confinement makes any threat inherently imminent. Without such confinement, the traditional strict imminence rule should apply.

(c) Argument-for: A student could contend that the Dingwall approach effectively replaces the temporal "imminence" requirement with a "credibility" inquiry. If the threat is credible based on past violent actions, the actual timing of the harm becomes secondary to the psychological pressure on the defendant. In this jurisdiction, once the threat is proven credible, the requirement that the harm be "immediate" is essentially eliminated in favor of a focus on the defendant's reasonable belief that they had no alternative.

(d) Argument-for: Because Dingwall and similar cases (like those involving Battered Woman Syndrome) arise out of specific power dynamics and domestic entrapment, one could argue that the legal exception is explicitly limited to that context. Since Holmes and Nietzsche were former associates in a criminal or business context rather than an intimate one, the "Dingwall exception" to strict imminence would not apply. This preserves the strict imminence requirement for standard criminal duress cases.

(e) Argument-for: Since the Dingwall approach relies on the psychological reality of a defendant living under "continuous and sustained" threats, a student might argue that expert testimony is a mandatory procedural bridge. Just as expert testimony is often required to establish the reasonableness of a defendant’s perception in BWS cases, the "Dingwall jurisdiction" would require such testimony to explain to a jury why a threat from an absent person is "imminent" before the defense can be submitted.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is the most accurate reflection of the Dingwall holding, which permits a jury instruction when a pattern of threats makes the danger seem "constant" and "unavoidable." The distractors all fail by using overly broad or false absolute restrictions. Option (b) is wrong because the very purpose of Dingwall is to address situations where the threatener is *not* present. Option (c) is wrong because the court still requires "imminence," just a redefined, non-temporal version of it; it does not "eliminate" the requirement. Option (d) is wrong because, while the case involved a domestic context, the Seventh Circuit’s reasoning applied to the duress defense generally. Option (e) is wrong because while expert testimony is helpful, no court has made it a "mandatory procedural precondition" for a duress instruction.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained" — wrong because Dingwall applied specifically to a defendant who was not physically confined by the threatener at the time of the crime.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because the court redefines rather than eliminates the element, still requiring a reasonable perception of "imminent" peril.
- (d): "explicitly limited to cases involving intimate partner violence" — wrong because the legal principle regarding sustained threats applies to the general duress defense.
- (e): "requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition" — wrong because Dingwall imposes no such evidentiary bar.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that has adopted the Dingwall reasoning on imminence in duress cases. Assume further that Nietzsche had been threatening Holmes for months, that Nietzsche had previously carried out threats against other people Holmes knew, and that Holmes reasonably believed local police were unable or unwilling to protect his mother. Would the duress analysis change? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because under the Dingwall approach, a pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the threatening party is not physically present at the time of the crime, and the combination of months of threats, Nietzsche's history of carrying out threats, and Holmes's reasonable belief that police cannot help would support submitting the duress claim to a jury. <!-- correct -->
(b) No, because the Dingwall reasoning relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained by the threatening party at the time of the crime, and Holmes was free to move about and had access to means of self-help.
(c) Yes, because the Dingwall approach eliminates the imminence requirement entirely and replaces it with a credibility inquiry, so any threat of future harm -- no matter how remote in time -- satisfies the duress defense if the threatening party has a track record of violence.
(d) No, because the *Dingwall* exception to strict imminence is explicitly limited to cases involving intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome, and the dispute between Holmes and his former associate Nietzsche falls outside that limitation.
(e) Yes, but only if Holmes can present expert testimony on the psychological effects of sustained threats, because the Dingwall approach requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition to submitting the duress claim to the jury.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** Under the *Dingwall* approach, imminence does not require physical proximity of the threatening party. A pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger, making a jury question appropriate under these modified facts. Option (b) falsely claims *Dingwall* applies only when the defendant is physically confined; *Dingwall* explicitly held physical presence is not required. Option (c) falsely asserts *Dingwall* eliminates the imminence requirement entirely; it relaxes it but does not eliminate it. Option (d) falsely limits the *Dingwall* reasoning to intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome; while *Dingwall* involved a battering relationship, its logic regarding how sustained threats satisfy imminence applies to duress generally and is not categorically restricted to IPV. Option (e) falsely states expert testimony is a mandatory procedural precondition; *Dingwall* imposes no such requirement.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students apply the strict physical proximity test even in circuits that have relaxed it, or limit Dingwall to IPV cases

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under *Dingwall*, the traditional strict physical proximity requirement for imminence is relaxed when there is a history of continuous and unpredictable threats, especially where police intervention is seen as futile. The reasoning is that the persistent nature of the threat creates a constant, looming danger that the defendant reasonably perceives as imminent. In this scenario, the months of threats, Nietzsche's track record, and the lack of police protection precisely mirror the psychological and factual conditions *Dingwall* recognized as sufficient to create a jury question on imminence. Therefore, (a) correctly applies the core logic of *Dingwall* to this continuous-threat scenario.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that while *Dingwall* relaxed strict temporal imminence, it still requires that the defendant lack any reasonable opportunity to escape. If a defendant is not physically confined or restrained, the traditional duress doctrine maintains that they must seek help or flee. Because Holmes was free to move about and had means of self-help, *Dingwall*'s narrow exception would arguably not apply, making (b) correct.
(c) Argument-for: One could argue that the practical effect of *Dingwall* is to shift the inquiry from a strict temporal "imminence" clock to an assessment of the threat's credibility based on the coercer's track record. If a track record of violence and months of threats are enough, then the imminence requirement is effectively absorbed into a credibility and reasonableness inquiry. Therefore, (c) accurately captures the realist view of what the *Dingwall* approach actually does: it substitutes strict imminence with a credibility test.
(d) Argument-for: *United States v. Dingwall* was heavily predicated on the specific dynamics of battering relationships and the admissibility of expert testimony regarding Battered Woman Syndrome. The Seventh Circuit's reasoning was deeply tied to how a batterer exerts control over an intimate partner. A student could strongly argue that this reasoning cannot simply be severed from the IPV context and applied to a standard criminal dispute between former associates, meaning the exception is functionally limited to those IPV facts.
(e) Argument-for: The *Dingwall* decision heavily emphasized the role of expert testimony in explaining why a battered woman would perceive a threat as imminent even when the abuser is absent. A student could argue that without such expert testimony, a lay jury cannot accurately assess the psychological effects of sustained threats. Therefore, extending the *Dingwall* approach requires expert testimony to bridge the gap between the objective absence of the threatener and the defendant's subjective perception of imminence.

Head-to-head: Option (a) correctly captures the substantive rule derived from *Dingwall* and applies it logically to the counterfactual. The distractors each present a plausible misunderstanding of the case's holding but rely on rigidly false legal limitations. Option (b) incorrectly mandates physical confinement. Option (c) goes too far by stating the imminence requirement is completely eliminated. Option (d) incorrectly claims the rule is "explicitly limited" to IPV cases, whereas the underlying reasoning on continuous threats is not categorically ring-fenced to that specific relationship type. Option (e) incorrectly elevates the helpfulness of expert testimony into a "mandatory procedural precondition." All distractors contain sufficiently locked, absolute falsifiable claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "relaxes the imminence requirement only for defendants who are physically confined or restrained" — wrong because *Dingwall* explicitly allows the defense even when the defendant is not physically confined or in the immediate presence of the threatener.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because *Dingwall* retains the imminence element but broadens how a continuous pattern of threats can satisfy it.
- (d): "explicitly limited to cases involving intimate partner violence or battered woman syndrome" — wrong because while the case involved a battering relationship, the court did not explicitly limit the legal reasoning regarding sustained threats to IPV contexts alone.
- (e): "requires such testimony as a mandatory procedural precondition" — wrong because expert testimony, while heavily discussed as admissible and helpful in *Dingwall*, is not a mandatory procedural prerequisite for raising the duress defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q06

### Grounding

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald. Based on the investigation, Markov struck Wald while acting under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was a government assassin trying to poison the residents. Afterward, Markov told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov understood he was physically striking a person, but because of an acute psychotic episode, he could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is therefore excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and, because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his act. (a) is incorrect because the "nature and quality" test refers to understanding the physical act itself, which Markov understood (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical nature of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness; his statement evidences his delusion. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not psychotic disorders. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses the "lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct" standard, not the "entirely destroyed" standard of the irresistible impulse test, and the facts establish a cognitive rather than volitional impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC Insanity (Section 4.01), Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald. Based on the investigation, Markov struck Wald while acting under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was a government assassin trying to poison the residents. Afterward, Markov told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov understood he was physically striking a person, but because of an acute psychotic episode, he could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is therefore excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives.
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and, because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his act. (a) is incorrect because the "nature and quality" test refers to understanding the physical act itself, which Markov understood (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical nature of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness; his statement evidences his delusion. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not psychotic disorders. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses the "lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct" standard, not the "entirely destroyed" standard of the irresistible impulse test, and the facts establish a cognitive rather than volitional impairment.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that the "nature and quality" of an act is not merely its physical trajectory but its social and legal character. If Markov believed he was striking a "government assassin" rather than a civilian, he arguably did not understand the "quality" of the act as an "assault," but rather as an act of justified defense. Because his delusion fundamentally altered his perception of the victim's identity, he did not know he was striking an "innocent person," which is the essential nature of the crime charged. Therefore, even under an MPC framework, the lack of appreciation for the victim's status satisfies the cognitive prong.

(b) Argument-for: This option focuses on the "substantial capacity" threshold of the MPC. A prosecutor would argue that Markov’s statement "I was stopping an assassination" proves he possessed a high level of cognitive functioning and awareness of his surroundings. By identifying his goal, Markov demonstrated that he was not in a state of total cognitive collapse. If a defendant can articulate a rational-sounding (though delusional) motive for his physical actions, he arguably retains the "substantial capacity" to understand that striking people is generally prohibited by law, making his defense a matter of "justification" rather than "insanity."

(c) Argument-for: Under a strict, literalist interpretation of MPC § 4.01(2), a jurisdiction could argue that if the primary evidence of Markov's "disease" is his history of violent outbursts or "antisocial conduct" like this assault, the condition is excluded. If Markov's schizophrenia primarily manifests through acts that violate the rights of others (assaults based on delusions), a court might find that the "disease" is defined solely by the conduct the law seeks to punish. This argument relies on the "caveat paragraph" intended to prevent the insanity defense from being used by those with personality disorders, extending it to any condition where the primary symptom is law-breaking.

(d) Argument-for: This is the most accurate application of the MPC "substantial capacity" test. Schizophrenia is a universally recognized mental disease. The MPC uses the term "appreciate" rather than the more restrictive "know," acknowledging that a defendant might have a superficial awareness of his actions while lacking an affective or moral understanding of their wrongfulness. Markov’s belief that he was saving residents from a poisoner demonstrates that he could not appreciate the moral or legal wrongfulness of the assault, as his internal reality provided a complete moral justification for the force used.

(e) Argument-for: This argument invokes the volitional prong of the MPC insanity defense. MPC § 4.01(1) excuses a defendant who lacks substantial capacity to "conform his conduct to the requirements of law." A defense expert could testify that Markov's acute psychotic episode created an overwhelming delusional imperative that made it impossible for him to resist the urge to "stop the assassin." If the schizophrenia "destroyed his capacity to control his behavior," he meets the volitional standard of the MPC, which is the modern successor to the "irresistible impulse" test.

Head-to-head: (d) is the strongest answer because it uses the exact MPC terminology ("lacks substantial capacity to appreciate") and correctly applies it to a cognitive delusion of justification. (a) is weak because "nature and quality" is M’Naghten language, not MPC language, and usually refers to physical nature (e.g., squeezing a neck thinking it's a lemon). (b) is weak because it uses a "therefore" bridge that is legally false; understanding the physical nature of an act does not automatically prove one appreciates its wrongfulness. (c) is weak because schizophrenia is a psychotic disorder, whereas the MPC exclusion is famously and categorically reserved for "antisocial personality disorder" (psychopathy). (e) is weak because it uses the "entirely destroyed" language of the discarded Irresistible Impulse test rather than the MPC’s "substantial capacity" standard, and it ignores that the facts more clearly support a cognitive rather than volitional impairment.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "he did not know the nature and quality of his act" — wrong because this is M'Naghten terminology, and Dr. Cole's testimony confirms Markov *did* understand the physical nature of his act (striking a person).
- (b): "demonstrates that he... therefore knew what he was doing was wrong" — wrong because knowing the physical nature of an act does not automatically/categorically establish an appreciation of its moral or legal wrongfulness.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia is a condition 'manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct' and is therefore excluded" — wrong because schizophrenia is a qualifying mental disease and is categorically not the "antisocial conduct" exclusion described in MPC 4.01(2).
- (e): "satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself" — wrong because the MPC does not use the "irresistible impulse test" (which requires total loss of control) but rather the "substantial capacity to conform" standard.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q6.** Your supervisor now turns to Markov's case (Fact 2). Markov is charged with second-degree assault for attacking Wald. Based on the investigation, Markov struck Wald while acting under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was a government assassin trying to poison the residents. Afterward, Markov told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole is prepared to testify that Markov understood he was physically striking a person, but because of an acute psychotic episode, he could not appreciate that his actions were wrong. Under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the nature and quality of his act -- he thought he was stopping an assassination, so he did not understand that he was striking an innocent person.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police -- "I was stopping an assassination" -- demonstrates that he understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is a condition "manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct" and is therefore excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect under MPC Section 4.01(2).
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct -- he understood he was striking a person but believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his behavior, satisfying the irresistible impulse test -- he could not have stopped himself from attacking Wald even if he had known the attack was wrong.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and, because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his act. (a) is incorrect because the "nature and quality" test refers to understanding the physical act itself, which Markov understood (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical nature of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness; his statement evidences his delusion. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC § 4.01(2) exclusion targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not psychotic disorders. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses the "lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct" standard, not the "entirely destroyed" standard of the irresistible impulse test, and the facts establish a cognitive rather than volitional impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under a broad reading of the cognitive prong of the insanity defense, "nature and quality" refers not just to the physical mechanics of an act but to its broader real-world significance. If Markov thought he was defending against an assassin, he fundamentally misunderstood the "quality" of striking an innocent person. A student might argue that this misunderstanding satisfies the legal test, making (a) an accurate description of his cognitive failure.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that if Markov knew he was stopping a person through violence ("I was stopping an assassination"), he inherently understood the act violated societal norms against assault. Under strict interpretations of wrongfulness, subjective moral justifications (believing the victim is an assassin) do not negate the knowledge that striking someone is legally and factually wrong. Therefore, (b) appeals to the notion that his statement proves an awareness of the physical and legal reality of his actions.
(c) Argument-for: MPC Section 4.01(2) explicitly excludes abnormalities manifested "only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct." A student could argue that because Markov's schizophrenia manifested in a violent, anti-social attack on Wald, it triggers this exclusion. If the student incorrectly maps all violent manifestations of mental illness to this psychopathy exclusion, (c) provides a textually grounded distractor.
(d) Argument-for: This option directly tracks the Model Penal Code standard in § 4.01. Markov has a mental disease (schizophrenia) that causes a cognitive impairment (he "could not appreciate that his actions were wrong"). Because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives from an assassin, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct, making this the textbook application of the MPC to the facts.
(e) Argument-for: The MPC contains a volitional prong—lacking substantial capacity to conform conduct to the law. A student might conflate the MPC's volitional prong with the traditional "irresistible impulse" test, arguing that Markov's psychotic episode destroyed his ability to control his behavior. If the delusion was so powerful that he had to act to stop the "assassin," a student could conclude this mimics an irresistible impulse, making (e) a plausible explanation.

Head-to-head: (d) correctly applies the MPC's "substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness" standard to Markov's cognitive delusion. Distractor (a) is incorrect because "nature and quality" is M'Naghten terminology, not MPC, and factually, Dr. Cole testifies Markov understood he was physically striking a person. Distractor (b) is wrong because it establishes a false legal equivalency, claiming that understanding the physical nature of an act automatically equates to knowing it is wrong. Distractor (c) relies on a clear misstatement of law, falsely defining schizophrenia as a condition manifested *only* by antisocial conduct, which misapplies the psychopathy exclusion. Distractor (e) fails because it invokes the wrong legal test ("irresistible impulse") and standard ("entirely destroyed") for an MPC jurisdiction. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "he did not know the nature and quality of his act" — wrong because Dr. Cole expressly testifies Markov "understood he was physically striking a person," meaning he understood the physical nature/quality of the act.
- (b): "demonstrates that he understood the physical nature of his actions and therefore knew what he was doing was wrong" — wrong because under the MPC, understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically mean the defendant has the capacity to appreciate its wrongfulness.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia is a condition 'manifested only by repeated antisocial conduct'" — wrong because schizophrenia is a classic psychotic mental disease, not the psychopathic/antisocial personality disorder excluded by MPC § 4.01(2).
- (e): "satisfying the irresistible impulse test" and "entirely destroyed his capacity" — wrong because the jurisdiction uses the MPC framework (substantial capacity), not the stricter common law irresistible impulse test.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q07

### Grounding

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: IDRA uses "lacked substantial capacity" — wrong because IDRA uses "unable," a stricter standard
(b) False claim: IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong — wrong because IDRA eliminated the volitional prong, not the wrongfulness prong
(c) False claim: IDRA places burden on prosecution — wrong because IDRA shifted burden to defendant
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: "Severe" excludes paranoid schizophrenia — wrong because schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic severe mental disease
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — federal IDRA vs MPC insanity standards, Ch 23, Refinement `federal-idra-requirements` and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

### Edge Case Audit

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold.

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: IDRA uses "lacked substantial capacity" — wrong because IDRA uses "unable," a stricter standard
(b) False claim: IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong — wrong because IDRA eliminated the volitional prong, not the wrongfulness prong
(c) False claim: IDRA places burden on prosecution — wrong because IDRA shifted burden to defendant
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: "Severe" excludes paranoid schizophrenia — wrong because schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic severe mental disease
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the IDRA and MPC tests are functionally identical in cases involving profound psychosis. While the IDRA uses the word "unable" and the MPC uses "substantial capacity," both focus on the defendant's cognitive appreciation of wrongfulness. In Markov's case, if his delusions are powerful enough to satisfy the MPC standard, they are likely powerful enough to meet the IDRA standard as well, making the linguistic difference purely academic. Thus, for Markov specifically, the change would make "no difference" in the ultimate outcome of the analysis.

(b) Argument-for: This option appeals to a student who understands that the IDRA was a restrictive reaction to the Hinckley acquittal. One way to restrict the insanity defense is to move toward a strict M'Naghten "nature and quality" test while stripping away the "moral wrongfulness" component. If IDRA were interpreted as a strict return to the cognitive-understanding-of-physical-acts standard, Markov would fail because he knew he was pulling a trigger and killing a human being. A student might believe the "reform" in the Act's title refers to this specific narrowing.

(c) Argument-for: This option targets students who confuse the federal IDRA with the pre-1984 federal standard. Under the old *Davis* rule, once a defendant raised a glimmer of insanity, the burden shifted to the prosecution to disprove it beyond a reasonable doubt. A student might argue that "the federal standard" generally favors the constitutional principle of placing all burdens on the government. If this standard applied, Markov's defense would be significantly "easier" to maintain in the face of prosecutorial rebuttal.

(d) Argument-for: This is the strongest case because it systematically identifies all four major shifts in the law triggered by 18 U.S.C. § 17. It correctly identifies the "severe" requirement, the elimination of the volitional prong (the "irresistible impulse" type test), the move from "substantial capacity" to a total "unable" standard, and the heightened burden of proof (clear and convincing). It then applies these to the facts, noting that even with these hurdles, a diagnosis like paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychosis is the classic candidate for a successful insanity defense.

(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that the addition of the word "severe" in the IDRA was the most critical change, as it was specifically intended to prevent "designer" defenses or personality disorders from qualifying. The student might contend that "severe" is a term of art that excludes most conditions except those where the defendant is truly non-functional. Under such a strict interpretation, Markov's ability to plan and execute a complex task might suggest he was not "incapacitated" enough to meet the "severe" threshold, thus making the defense harder "solely" on that basis.

Head-to-head: (d) is the superior answer because it provides an exhaustive and accurate list of the four technical changes in the federal statute. (a) contains a false legal claim regarding the phrasing of the IDRA cognitive test. (b) contains a false legal claim regarding the elimination of the wrongfulness prong; IDRA explicitly includes the inability to appreciate the "wrongfulness" of acts. (c) contains a false legal claim regarding the burden of proof, which IDRA famously shifted to the defendant. (e) contains a false claim that the "severe" requirement categorically excludes paranoid schizophrenia; in fact, the legislative history and subsequent case law make clear that such psychotic disorders are precisely what the "severe" requirement was meant to include, while excluding personality disorders like sociopathy.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness'" — wrong because IDRA uses the stricter "unable to appreciate" standard.
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because IDRA explicitly retains the "wrongfulness" prong in 18 U.S.C. § 17(a).
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17(b)) explicitly places the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "IDRA adds the word 'severe'... which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia" — wrong because paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychosis is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease for which the IDRA was designed.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal IDRA standard rather than the MPC. Your supervisor asks how this would change the analysis (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct -- and Markov satisfies both.

(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act, which Markov understood.

(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant.

(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires that the mental disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and requires proof by clear and convincing evidence rather than preponderance -- but Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes would likely satisfy even the stricter IDRA threshold. <!-- correct -->

(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. Section 17) differs from the MPC in four key ways: (1) the mental disease must be "severe," (2) the defendant must have been "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (not merely lacking "substantial capacity"), (3) no volitional prong exists, and (4) the defendant must prove the defense by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's case is strong even under IDRA: paranoid schizophrenia with command hallucinations is a "severe" mental disease, and an acute psychotic episode in which the defendant genuinely believes he is stopping an assassination is likely sufficient to show he was "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness. Option (a) states that both tests use "identical cognitive tests" including "lacked substantial capacity" -- the false proposition is that IDRA uses "substantial capacity." IDRA uses "unable," which is a meaningfully higher bar than the MPC's "lacks substantial capacity." Option (b) states that IDRA "eliminates the wrongfulness prong" -- the false proposition is this elimination. IDRA retains both the nature-and-quality prong and the wrongfulness prong; it is the volitional prong that was eliminated. Option (c) states that IDRA "places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity" -- the false proposition is this burden allocation. IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant, requiring proof by clear and convincing evidence. Option (e) states that IDRA's "severe" requirement "excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total incapacitation" -- the false proposition is that paranoid schizophrenia does not meet the severity threshold. Paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic "severe" mental disease.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: IDRA uses "lacked substantial capacity" — wrong because IDRA uses "unable," a stricter standard
(b) False claim: IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong — wrong because IDRA eliminated the volitional prong, not the wrongfulness prong
(c) False claim: IDRA places burden on prosecution — wrong because IDRA shifted burden to defendant
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: "Severe" excludes paranoid schizophrenia — wrong because schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is the paradigmatic severe mental disease
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: IDRA and MPC both contain a cognitive prong centered on "appreciating wrongfulness." A student might argue that the slight linguistic distinction between "lacked substantial capacity" (MPC) and "unable" (IDRA) is a distinction without a practical difference in many cognitive-insanity cases, especially in jurisdictions that read "unable" functionally as a substantial lack of capacity. Therefore, practically speaking, they use identical cognitive tests, and Markov satisfies both.
(b) Argument-for: Under the original M'Naghten rule, some jurisdictions limited insanity strictly to the "nature and quality" of the act. A student might mistakenly believe that IDRA, being a strict post-Hinckley reform, reverted entirely to the strictest form of M'Naghten, eliminating the wrongfulness prong to prevent defendants who knew what they were doing from using the defense. Thus, under this view, IDRA defeats Markov entirely.
(c) Argument-for: Prior to IDRA, the federal rule placed the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt once raised. A student might argue that this historical fact still governs constitutional due process under *In re Winship* for elements of culpability, and thus IDRA actually kept or reinforced this burden for cognitive insanity, making the defense easier than some state versions of the MPC.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly identifies all the major statutory changes enacted by the IDRA: adding "severe," eliminating the volitional ("irresistible impulse") prong, changing "substantial capacity" to "unable," and shifting the burden of proof to the defendant under a clear and convincing evidence standard. Despite these heightened requirements, paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes is precisely the type of severe mental illness IDRA was designed to cover, meaning Markov's defense remains viable.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) correctly recognizes that IDRA added the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement. A student might argue that this addition was specifically intended to filter out all but the most incapacitating disorders, leaving out common episodic disorders like schizophrenia unless they cause total permanent incapacitation. Under this strict reading, IDRA's primary mechanism of making the defense harder is this definitional exclusion.

Head-to-head: Option (d) accurately recites the specific statutory mechanics of the federal IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) and applies them correctly to the facts. The other options fail because they rely on definitively false statements about the IDRA statute's plain text or application. Option (a) incorrectly claims the IDRA uses "substantial capacity," which it explicitly does not. Option (b) incorrectly claims IDRA eliminated the wrongfulness prong, whereas it actually eliminated the volitional prong. Option (c) completely reverses the post-Hinckley burden of proof, claiming the prosecution must disprove it. Option (e) falsely uses the absolute phrase "solely because" and wrongly claims paranoid schizophrenia is excluded from being a "severe" mental disease. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests -- both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity...'" — wrong because IDRA explicitly uses the standard "unable to appreciate," rejecting the MPC's "substantial capacity."
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because IDRA expressly retained the cognitive wrongfulness prong (whether the defendant was unable to appreciate the nature and quality or the wrongfulness of his acts).
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because the IDRA shifted the burden of proof to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "solely because IDRA adds the word 'severe'... which excludes common psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia" — wrong because IDRA made several other changes (burden, volitional prong), and paranoid schizophrenia is canonically recognized as a severe mental disease under the statute.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q08

### Grounding

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.
(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the required mental state -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->
(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model, the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required mental state. However, Markov deliberately struck Wald, intending physical contact and harm, so his claim relies on not knowing the strike was wrong—which is irrelevant under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief; evidence is only permitted to negate a statutory element. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence solely bearing on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Mens Rea Model Abolition, Ch 23, Refinement `mens-rea-model-abolition` and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->
