### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.
(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the required mental state -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->
(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model, the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required mental state. However, Markov deliberately struck Wald, intending physical contact and harm, so his claim relies on not knowing the strike was wrong—which is irrelevant under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief; evidence is only permitted to negate a statutory element. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence solely bearing on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that "abolishing the insanity defense" signals a total legislative rejection of psychiatric excuses. In such a regime, the court might rule that mental illness evidence is per se confusing to a jury or lacks a scientific nexus to the objective "intent to strike" required by an assault statute. If the jurisdiction views psychiatric testimony as an attempt to re-introduce the abolished insanity defense through the back door, it might categorically bar such records to preserve the legislative intent of abolition. Under this strict "no-defense" view, Markov would be prevented from introducing any mental health evidence during the guilt phase.

(b) Argument-for: This option rests on the theory of "fundamental principles of justice" inherent in the Due Process Clause. An advocate could argue that the common law's centuries-long commitment to the M'Naghten rule created a constitutional floor that states cannot drop below. Because the ability to distinguish right from wrong is central to the concept of "mens rea" or "moral blameworthiness" in Western law, the Supreme Court's jurisprudence (prior to or ignoring the specific holding of Kahler) could be read to mandate a wrongfulness inquiry. This argument posits that a state cannot constitutionally convict a defendant who, due to mental illness, lacks the capacity to perceive the moral or legal wrongfulness of his acts.

(c) Argument-for: This is the legally accurate description of the mens rea model upheld in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Under this model, the affirmative defense of insanity is gone, but the prosecution still bears the burden of proving every element of the crime, including the mental state. Markov can introduce psychiatric evidence, but only for the narrow purpose of showing he did not actually possess the "purpose" or "knowledge" to cause bodily injury. If he intended to strike the victim (even for a delusional reason), the evidence fails to negate the mens rea, and since the "wrongfulness" defense is abolished, he has no further legal recourse.

(d) Argument-for: One could argue that "mens rea" (guilty mind) inherently requires a lack of justification. If Markov believed he was acting in defense of others against an assassin, he lacked the "culpability" usually associated with assault. In this view, the mens rea model's allowance of mental illness evidence should logically extend to delusional beliefs that, if true, would constitute a legal justification. If the law allows a sane person to strike someone in defense of others, it should allow a mentally ill person to argue they lacked the "guilty mind" because they believed their actions were legally authorized or required.

(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that "abolition" is a misnomer and that mental health evidence, once admitted, cannot be surgically limited to the negation of elements. If a jurisdiction permits psychiatric testimony to show the defendant didn't "know" what he was doing, that testimony inevitably informs the jury's understanding of whether the defendant "knew" it was wrong. This argument suggests that as a practical and evidentiary matter, once the door to psychiatric evidence is open for mens rea, it is open for all purposes related to the defendant's responsibility, effectively preserving the wrongfulness inquiry under the guise of an element-negation defense.

Head-to-head: The conflict centers on the scope of the "mens rea model" as defined in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Option (c) is the strongest because it correctly identifies that the model limits evidence to the negation of statutory elements and excludes the "moral wrongfulness" inquiry. Option (b) is the weakest because it asserts a constitutional requirement that *Kahler* explicitly rejected. Option (a) overstates the effect of abolition by claiming a total evidentiary bar, which violates the defendant's right to contest the elements of the crime. Option (d) incorrectly invents a "freestanding" justification defense that is not part of the mens rea model. Option (e) incorrectly suggests that abolition states still permit wrongfulness inquiries, which is the exact definition of what they do NOT permit.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant... and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose." — wrong because *Kahler* confirms states must still allow defendants to use such evidence to contest the mens rea elements of the crime.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense." — wrong because *Kahler* held exactly the opposite: the Due Process Clause does not require a wrongfulness defense.
- (d): "delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated." — wrong because the mens rea model *only* allows mental health evidence to the extent it negates a statutory element; it provides no independent or freestanding defenses.
- (e): "abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes... including moral wrongfulness." — wrong because the hallmark of the mens rea model/abolition is the specific exclusion of the moral wrongfulness inquiry.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense at all because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense for mentally ill defendants.
(c) Markov could use his mental illness to negate the mens rea element of assault, but only if his expert testified that the schizophrenia prevented him from forming the required mental state -- for example, that the psychotic episode prevented him from acting purposely or knowingly with respect to causing physical injury. The wrongfulness of the act would be irrelevant. <!-- correct -->
(d) Markov would be acquitted under the mens rea model because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified -- since Markov believed he was defending others from an assassin, his delusional belief in justification provides a freestanding defense that operates independently of whether any statutory element is negated.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue that he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model, the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea element. Markov could argue his psychotic episode prevented him from forming the required mental state. However, Markov deliberately struck Wald, intending physical contact and harm, so his claim relies on not knowing the strike was wrong—which is irrelevant under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental illness evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler v. Kansas* held that the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on delusional belief; evidence is only permitted to negate a statutory element. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence solely bearing on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that if a state "abolishes" the insanity defense, the logical conclusion is that mental illness can no longer be raised at trial to excuse or justify the crime. Relying on *Montana v. Egelhoff*, they might reason that states have the power to entirely exclude certain types of exculpatory evidence. Therefore, expert testimony about Markov's mental state would be inadmissible for any purpose, leaving him with no defense at all.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that punishing someone who cannot tell right from wrong violates fundamental fairness and the history of the common law. They might recall that the Supreme Court recently addressed this constitutional question in *Kahler v. Kansas* and misremember the holding as establishing a due process floor that guarantees a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense.
(c) Argument-for: This accurately applies the mens rea model adopted by Kansas and upheld in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Under this model, the affirmative defense of insanity is abolished, but defendants must be allowed to introduce evidence of mental illness to negate the statutory mens rea elements of the crime. Markov can use evidence of his schizophrenia solely to show he did not act "purposely or knowingly," while his inability to understand the wrongfulness of his acts is legally irrelevant.
(d) Argument-for: A student could argue that self-defense or defense of others is a justification defense that relies on the defendant's subjective belief in the need for force. If Markov's mental illness caused a genuine, albeit delusional, belief that he was defending against an assassin, a student might reason that this satisfies the requirements for a freestanding justification defense, overriding the strict limits of the mens rea model.
(e) Argument-for: A student might argue that even if the formal "insanity defense" is abolished as a standalone verdict, the state must still evaluate criminal responsibility comprehensively. They could reason that evidence of mental illness remains admissible for all purposes bearing on culpability, meaning Markov could still introduce evidence of his inability to grasp moral wrongfulness.

Head-to-head: Option (c) correctly states the legal rule for states that have adopted the mens rea model: mental illness evidence is admissible to negate mens rea, but not to establish an affirmative defense based on a lack of knowledge of wrongfulness. All four distractors contain explicit false statements of law regarding the mens rea model or Supreme Court precedent. Distractor (a) falsely claims abolition states make mental illness evidence completely inadmissible. Distractor (b) misstates the direct holding of *Kahler v. Kansas*. Distractor (d) invents a rule that the mens rea model permits a freestanding complete defense for delusional justification. Distractor (e) directly contradicts the core operational mechanic of the mens rea model by claiming it permits evidence of moral wrongfulness.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings, and neither expert testimony nor psychiatric records would be admissible for any purpose" — wrong because the mens rea model allows mental illness evidence to negate statutory mens rea.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in Kahler v. Kansas that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" — wrong because *Kahler* held the exact opposite (states may constitutionally abolish the wrongfulness test).
- (d): "because the model permits a complete defense whenever the defendant's mental illness caused him to believe his conduct was justified" — wrong because the mens rea model abolishes insanity as an affirmative defense and does not allow delusional beliefs to create a freestanding justification defense.
- (e): "abolition states permit mental illness evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility, including moral wrongfulness" — wrong because abolition states strictly limit the evidence to negating mens rea and explicitly exclude it when offered to show a lack of moral wrongfulness.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q09

### Grounding

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

<!-- Self-check:
(a) False claim: MPC excludes any defendant with a personality disorder diagnosis — wrong because the exclusion applies only to conditions constituting solely a personality disorder
(b) False claim: MPC provides for automatic merger of co-occurring disorders — wrong because no such merger doctrine exists
(c) False claim: Co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies causation — wrong because the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity
(d) CORRECT
(e) False claim: But-for causation by the personality disorder defeats the defense — wrong because the statute does not impose a sole-cause requirement
-->

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC Personality Disorder Exclusion, Ch 23, Refinement `personality-disorder-exclusion`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

## The Question

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. ### Edge Case Audit

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." The word "solely" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but a personality disorder co-occurring with a qualifying mental illness does not disqualify the defense. The exclusion applies to the threshold requirement (is there a qualifying mental disease?), not to causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity -- even if the personality disorder also contributed -- the defense survives. Option (a) states that the MPC "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" -- the false proposition is this absolute bar. The MPC excludes conditions constituting *solely* a personality disorder; a defendant with both schizophrenia and a personality disorder is not excluded. Option (b) states that the MPC provides for "automatic merger" of co-occurring disorders into "one legal category" -- the false proposition is this merger doctrine, which does not exist. The disorders remain legally distinct; the question is which one caused the incapacity. Option (c) states that a co-occurring psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" -- the false proposition is this automatic satisfaction. The defense must still prove that the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity; the presence of a qualifying disease does not eliminate the causation inquiry. Option (e) states that the prosecution "need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause" to "defeat the defense entirely" -- the false proposition is this but-for causation standard. The statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause; if the schizophrenia caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: The "caveat paragraph" of MPC Section 4.01(2) is designed to exclude the "sociopath" or "psychopath" from the insanity defense to prevent character-based criminality from being excused. A proponent could argue that allowing a defendant with a personality disorder diagnosis to raise the defense, even with a co-occurring illness, creates an unacceptable risk that the jury will excuse behavior driven by antisocial traits. To safeguard the policy goal of the exclusion, the rule must be interpreted as an absolute bar for any defendant carrying such a diagnosis, ensuring that the defense is reserved for those whose impairment is untainted by character-based disorders.

(b) Argument-for: Medical science often views co-morbidities not as discrete boxes but as a single, integrated psychiatric profile. In the interest of judicial economy and medical accuracy, a proponent could argue that the MPC implicitly adopts a "merger" doctrine. Under this view, when a qualifying disease (schizophrenia) and a non-qualifying disorder (APD) co-occur, they "automatically merge" into a single legal category of "mental disease or defect." This prevents the prosecution from engaging in the impossible task of disentangling which specific neuron "caused" the violence, treating the defendant's mental state as a unified whole.

(c) Argument-for: Schizophrenia is a severe psychotic disorder that fundamentally disrupts a defendant's cognitive and volitional faculties. A proponent could argue that because psychotic disorders are the "gold standard" for the insanity defense, their presence should "trump" any secondary diagnosis. Under this logic, once schizophrenia is established, it "automatically satisfies" the causation element because a psychotic break is legally presumed to be the primary driver of behavior. This makes the prosecution's focus on the personality disorder "legally irrelevant" because the higher-order qualifying disease dictates the legal outcome.

(d) Argument-for: This is the correct legal standard. MPC 4.01(2) defines what counts as a "mental disease or defect" by exclusion, stating that the term does not include an abnormality manifested "only" by repeated criminal conduct. This is a threshold inquiry. If the defendant has schizophrenia, they have met the threshold for a qualifying disease. The causation question then asks if *that* disease caused the lack of capacity. The law does not require the qualifying disease to be the exclusive cause; as long as it is a sufficient cause of the incapacity, the presence of a contributing personality disorder does not defeat the defense.

(e) Argument-for: In criminal law, causation is typically established using the but-for test. A proponent could argue that for an insanity defense to be valid, the *qualifying* mental disease must be the necessary cause of the criminal act. If the prosecution can demonstrate that the defendant's antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause—meaning the violence would not have occurred without the antisocial traits—then the defense fails. Because the personality disorder is a "non-qualifying" cause, its status as a but-for factor creates an "insurmountable causation problem" that defeats the defense entirely.

Head-to-head: (d) is the strongest because it correctly interprets the word "only" in the MPC text as a threshold gatekeeper rather than a causation-disqualifier. (a) is legally incorrect because the MPC excludes an "abnormality," not a "defendant." (b) is incorrect because there is no "merger" doctrine in the MPC or case law; disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because causation is always a question of fact for the jury, and no diagnosis "automatically" satisfies it. (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not require the qualifying disease to be the *sole* cause; the test is whether the qualifying disease resulted in the lack of capacity, regardless of other contributing factors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense" — wrong because the exclusion applies to abnormalities manifested *solely* by conduct, not to defendants who also have qualifying diseases.
- (b): "MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because no such merger doctrine exists in the MPC or insanity jurisprudence.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because causation remains a distinct element that the defense must prove as a matter of fact.
- (e): "prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause... to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the presence of a contributory non-qualifying cause does not negate the causal impact of a qualifying disease.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness.

(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient.

(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which disorder "drove" the behavior legally irrelevant.

(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement -- if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC Section 4.01(2), a condition constituting "solely a personality disorder" does not qualify as a "mental disease or defect." **Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Option (a) is correct by reading the MPC § 4.01(2) "psychopath exception" as an absolute, tainting bar. If a jurisdiction treats an antisocial personality disorder diagnosis as per se evidence of an excluded abnormality manifested by repeated criminal conduct, the presence of that disorder would trigger a complete statutory bar to prevent the "bad rather than mad" from utilizing the defense. Thus, conceding the defeat of the defense is the safest response, regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct by treating co-occurring disorders as inseparable clinical realities. In mental health law, separating the exact causal contribution of intertwined mental conditions is notoriously difficult. A student might argue that under the MPC's holistic assessment of mental disease or defect, comorbid diagnoses automatically merge into a single clinical picture, making the prosecution's attempt to parse the personality disorder out legally invalid.
(c) Argument-for: Option (c) could be defended by asserting that a severe psychotic disorder like schizophrenia operates as a "trump" condition. Under this theory, once a defendant establishes a qualifying severe psychotic disorder, the causation element is presumptively met because psychosis inherently overwhelms other psychological mechanisms. Therefore, the prosecution's argument about the personality disorder driving the behavior is legally irrelevant once the threshold of a severe psychotic disease is passed.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly states the legal standard under MPC § 4.01(2). The MPC provides that the terms "mental disease or defect" do not include an abnormality manifested *only* by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct. Because the statute targets a condition acting *alone*, a co-occurring qualifying disease like schizophrenia allows the defendant to pass the threshold requirement. The defense must then show that the qualifying disease caused the substantial incapacity, which remains a factual question for the jury.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) could be defended by applying strict but-for causation principles to the insanity defense's exclusions. If the antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct, the student might argue that the behavior was inherently "driven" by the excluded condition, thus tainting the causal chain. Under this view, any essential causal contribution by an excluded disorder defeats the defense entirely because the incapacity was not exclusively caused by the qualifying mental disease.

Head-to-head:
Option (d) is unequivocally the strongest argument because it faithfully applies the MPC § 4.01(2) text, which only excludes an abnormality manifested "only" (or "solely") by repeated criminal conduct. The distractors fail because they rely on fabricated absolutes that contradict the MPC framework. Option (a) falsely claims the MPC categorically excludes any defendant with a personality disorder diagnosis. Option (b) invents an "automatic merger" doctrine for co-occurring disorders. Option (c) falsely asserts that a psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, ignoring the ongoing burden to prove causation. Option (e) falsely claims that but-for causation by a personality disorder defeats the defense entirely, whereas the MPC permits the defense to survive if the qualifying disease caused the incapacity, even if other factors contributed.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness" — wrong because the MPC exclusion targets abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; a co-occurring qualifying illness lifts the threshold bar.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because no such automatic merger doctrine exists in the MPC.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defense must still independently prove that the qualifying mental disease caused the substantial incapacity.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the MPC does not impose a sole-cause requirement; concurrent causation by a personality disorder does not legally preclude the defense.

Recommended fix: none

## Q10

### Grounding

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law—a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family member's). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress (Murder Bar), Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar`. Rule paraphrased accurately. Distractors are supported by refinements `duress-target-scope` and `felony-murder-duress-exception`. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law—a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family member's). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law—a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family members). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the distinction between an excuse and a justification. While the common law historically refused to fully "justify" the taking of an innocent life, many jurisdictions allow the high level of coercion to serve as a mitigating factor. By framing the "murder bar" as a sentencing limitation rather than a guilt-phase exclusion, a court could preserve the moral sanctity of life while acknowledging the psychological reality of Holmes's position. Given the credible threat to a close relative, this interpretation allows the jury to consider the defendant's lack of free will.

(b) Argument-for: This is the traditional, "black-letter" common law rule as articulated by Blackstone. The common law views the duty to avoid killing an innocent person as higher than the duty of self-preservation. Because murder is a crime of such gravity, the law demands that a person undergo death rather than commit it. Since Fact 6 specifies that the common law applies, and Fact 5 involves a death, the defense is legally unavailable regardless of the threat's severity or the victim's identity.

(c) Argument-for: This argument appeals to the modern evolution of criminal law. The Model Penal Code (MPC § 2.09) famously abandoned the common law's homicide exception, arguing that it is unfair to punish someone for failing to meet a standard of heroism that a person of "reasonable firmness" could not achieve. By asserting a "modern trend," this option argues that the common law has evolved to incorporate MPC-style fairness, making the defense available in all jurisdictions to prevent the conviction of the morally blameless.

(d) Argument-for: This position relies on a strict, originalist interpretation of the common law. Early common law requirements for duress were extremely narrow, requiring an immediate threat of death or serious bodily harm to the actor's own person. By arguing that the common law excludes threats against third parties, even close relatives, this analysis provides a secondary reason (in addition to the homicide bar) why Holmes's defense would fail, emphasizing the rigorous restrictive nature of the defense's elements.

(e) Argument-for: This argument uses a "derivative liability" logic. For felony murder to apply, the defendant must be guilty of the underlying predicate felony. If Holmes committed the predicate assault under duress, the duress excuses the assault. If the assault is excused, it cannot serve as the "felony" in "felony murder." Therefore, by negating the predicate crime, duress effectively "breaks the chain" of liability for the resulting death, a principle purportedly applied universally across jurisdictions.

Head-to-head: (b) is the strongest answer because it correctly identifies the absolute common law bar against using duress as a defense to murder. (a) is incorrect because the common law murder bar is a substantive exclusion from the defense itself, not merely a sentencing consideration. (c) is incorrect because it ignores the Fact 6 prompt (Common Law) and makes an objectively false claim that the defense is extended to homicide in "all" jurisdictions (many still follow the CL bar). (d) is incorrect because the common law long ago expanded to include threats against a defendant's family. (e) is incorrect because it uses the absolute terms "always" and "every jurisdiction" regarding the felony murder exception; in many jurisdictions, duress to a predicate felony does not excuse a resulting death.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase." — wrong because the common law bar is an absolute substantive prohibition on raising the defense for murder.
- (c): "modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions." — wrong because most U.S. jurisdictions (and many common law countries) still maintain the murder exclusion.
- (d): "common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised." — wrong because the common law defense extends to threats against close family members.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability... in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense." — wrong because several jurisdictions explicitly deny the duress defense for the homicide even if it would have been available for the underlying felony.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder under the common law—a defendant may not take another life to save her own or a family member's life, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. <!-- correct -->

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the common law, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to murder. The rationale is that a defendant may not take another life to save their own (or a family member's). This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: At early common law, strict rules often forced defendants to rely on executive clemency or judicial discretion at sentencing when faced with extreme duress. A student might interpret this historical reality to mean that the "murder bar" is not an absolute denial of the defense's relevance, but rather a mechanism that shifts the mitigation to the sentencing phase. If the bar only limits complete exoneration, the defendant effectively retains a "defense" that limits exposure. Therefore, option (a) provides a sophisticated procedural reinterpretation of the common law rule.
(b) Argument-for: This option correctly reflects the foundational common law rule derived from Blackstone, which establishes that one cannot take the life of an innocent person to save oneself. The underlying rationale is the absolute sanctity and equality of human life, dictating that a person must sacrifice their own life rather than commit murder. This serves as an absolute, categorical bar to raising duress for a murder charge. Consequently, even an objectively credible threat of death to a defendant or their family member cannot overcome this substantive guilt-phase bar.
(c) Argument-for: Section 2.09 of the Model Penal Code intentionally eliminated the categorical common law murder bar, substituting a "person of reasonable firmness" standard that applies to all crimes. A student could argue that modern common law courts have absorbed this MPC innovation to avoid the harshness of the traditional rule. Under this view, the MPC reflects a prevailing modern trend that actively extends the defense to homicide. This makes option (c) attractive as a progressive, contemporary synthesis of duress doctrine.
(d) Argument-for: The classic formulation of duress strictly requires an imminent and impending threat of death or great bodily harm. A formalist reading suggests this requirement was intensely personal, historically meant to excuse conduct only when the defendant's own immediate survival instinct overbore their will. Under such a strict interpretation, a threat to a third party—even a mother—would fail the personal-target requirement. Thus, option (d) logically applies a rigid reading of common law imminence and target requirements.
(e) Argument-for: In felony murder, the underlying predicate felony is what supplies the requisite malice for the homicide charge. If duress perfectly negates the mens rea or culpability for the predicate felony (such as an assault), then legally there is no underlying felony to trigger the felony murder rule. A student could logically deduce that duress must systematically break the chain of liability because the foundational element of the felony murder doctrine has been destroyed. Therefore, option (e) offers a compelling, doctrinally consistent rationale for negating the murder charge.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the correct application of the traditional common law rule, which categorically prohibits duress as an affirmative defense to murder. Option (a) invents a procedural mechanism, falsely classifying the substantive common law bar as merely a sentencing-phase consideration. Option (c) misapplies the MPC to a question explicitly asking for the common law and falsely asserts a universal modern trend in "all jurisdictions." Option (d) incorrectly states that the common law requires the threat to target the defendant personally, when in fact threats to close family members are widely recognized as sufficient. Option (e) presents a logical but legally inaccurate absolute, falsely claiming that duress "always" breaks the chain of felony murder in "every jurisdiction," ignoring states that apply the murder bar to felony murder as well.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase, not merely a sentencing consideration.
- (c): "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because "in all jurisdictions" is an explicit false absolute; many jurisdictions retain the traditional categorical bar against duress for murder.
- (d): "requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law explicitly extends the duress defense to threats of death or serious bodily injury directed at the defendant's close relatives.
- (e): "always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction" — wrong because it relies on the absolute false claims "always" and "every jurisdiction"; multiple jurisdictions prohibit duress from serving as a defense to felony murder.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q11

### Grounding

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break outweighs the property damage, and the unavailability of an ER bed satisfies the no-legal-alternative element.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — necessity and fault, Ch 21, Refinement `mpc-fault-calibration` and `legislative-preemption-absolute`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->
