### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break outweighs the property damage, and the unavailability of an ER bed satisfies the no-legal-alternative element.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: In *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative*, the Supreme Court held that the necessity defense is unavailable when the legislature has already weighed the competing values and made a choice. By creating a comprehensive regulatory scheme for controlled substances (the CSA) and omitting a medical necessity exception, the legislature has effectively preempted the judicial application of the necessity defense for drug offenses. Because the drugs Kant possessed are scheduled, the "choice of evils" has already been resolved by the legislature against him, rendering the defense categorically unavailable regardless of the specific medical emergency.

(b) Argument-for: Kant’s defense is viable under both standards because he satisfies the core requirements of necessity: proportionality and the lack of alternatives. A "psychotic break" represents a severe, irreparable psychological injury that arguably outweighs the temporary harm of property damage and drug possession. Furthermore, the lack of an available ER bed demonstrates that Kant had no legal alternative to prevent the harm. Since the common law is ultimately a standard of reasonableness, a jury could find that preventing mental collapse justifies the minor criminal infractions despite his prior negligence.

(c) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the distinction between the common law "clean hands" requirement and the MPC’s fault-calibration rule. Common law jurisdictions generally bar the necessity defense if the defendant was at fault in creating the emergency. Under MPC § 3.02(2), however, prior recklessness or negligence only bars the defense for crimes for which those same mental states suffice for conviction. Since Kant was charged with intentional and knowing crimes, the MPC preserves the defense, making this the most legally accurate description of the split.

(d) Argument-for: MPC § 3.02(2) states that the justification of necessity is "unavailable" when the actor is reckless or negligent in bringing about the situation. A student could argue that this provision serves as a threshold requirement for the defense. If the defendant's own conduct is the source of the emergency, the moral justification for violating the law is undermined regardless of the specific mens rea of the crime charged. Therefore, Kant’s failure to refill his prescription acts as a categorical bar under the MPC's fault provision.

(e) Argument-for: Common law necessity has historically been interpreted narrowly, often requiring a threat of physical injury or death to justify a criminal act. Because "necessity" is a residual defense of last resort, many jurisdictions restrict its application to "imminent" threats of serious bodily harm or death. Since a psychotic break is a psychological condition rather than an immediate physical threat to life, a court applying strict common-law standards would find the defense inapplicable, whereas the more flexible MPC "choice of evils" standard would allow it.

Head-to-head: (c) is the correct answer because it accurately reflects the "fault-calibration" rule in MPC § 3.02(2) and the "clean hands" rule in common law. (a) is incorrect because *Oakland Cannabis* focused on the specific lack of medical benefit for Schedule I substances; it does not categorically preempt necessity for all prescription drugs which, by definition, have accepted medical use. (b) fails because it ignores the common-law rule that any fault in creating the emergency bars the defense. (d) is incorrect because it ignores the second half of MPC § 3.02(2), which limits the bar to crimes where recklessness or negligence suffices for culpability. (e) is incorrect because it erroneously imports the "death or serious bodily injury" requirement from the defense of duress into the necessity defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption" — wrong because *Oakland Cannabis* relied on the specific Congressional finding that Schedule I drugs have *no* medical use; prescription drugs (Schedules II-V) have recognized medical uses.
- (b): "The defense is viable under both standards" — wrong because common law categorically bars the defense if the defendant was at fault in creating the emergency.
- (d): "§ 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent" — wrong because § 3.02(2) only strips the defense for crimes where recklessness/negligence suffices for culpability.
- (e): "common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because necessity (unlike duress) is a balancing test that can apply to any lesser evil, including property or psychological harm.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break outweighs the property damage, and the unavailability of an ER bed satisfies the no-legal-alternative element.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. <!-- correct -->
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: *Oakland Cannabis* stands for the proposition that when a legislature schedules a drug and regulates its distribution, it has already balanced the medical benefits against the harms, thereby preempting the necessity defense. Because Kant is seeking a controlled substance, the legislative scheme for prescriptions arguably occupies the field. Therefore, a student could conclude that any necessity defense for stealing a controlled substance is barred by legislative preemption.
(b) Argument-for: Necessity requires that the harm avoided be greater than the harm caused and that there be no legal alternatives. Averting a psychotic break is arguably a greater harm than the property damage of breaking into a pharmacy. A student could argue that if the substantive balance of harms weighs in the defendant's favor and there are no alternatives, the defense should be viable under both the MPC and the common law, overriding the fact that his prescription expired.
(c) Argument-for: This accurately reflects the divergence between the Model Penal Code and the common law on the fault element. Traditional common law features a strict "clean hands" requirement, barring the necessity defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency. In contrast, MPC § 3.02(2) calibrates fault to the mens rea of the charged crime. Because Kant is charged with "intentional" and "knowing" crimes, his negligence or recklessness in letting his prescription expire does not bar the defense under the MPC.
(d) Argument-for: MPC § 3.02 does limit the availability of the necessity defense when the defendant is at fault for creating the crisis. A student might remember that fault plays a limiting role and reasonably (but incorrectly) deduce that the MPC, like the traditional common law, acts as a complete, categorical bar to the justification defense whenever the defendant's recklessness or negligence brings about the emergency.
(e) Argument-for: The common law often imposes stricter requirements on justification defenses than the MPC. For instance, the common-law duress defense traditionally requires an imminent threat of death or serious bodily injury. A student might conflate necessity with duress and conclude that the common-law necessity defense similarly restricts the averted harm strictly to threats of death or serious bodily harm, rendering the defense unavailable for merely preventing a psychotic break.

Head-to-head: Option (c) is the only legally accurate claim, properly identifying the common law's categorical clean-hands rule and the MPC's nuanced mens rea calibration. Option (a) overstates *Oakland Cannabis*, which relied on a specific statutory finding that Schedule I drugs have no accepted medical use, not the mere existence of a scheduling system. Option (b) falsely claims the defense is viable under the common law, ignoring the fault bar; it also relies on an extraneous fact not in the prompt ("unavailability of an ER bed"). Option (d) directly contradicts MPC § 3.02(2) by asserting a categorical bar instead of a calibrated one. Option (e) imports the "death or serious bodily injury" requirement from duress into necessity, which is legally false. All distractors present clear, falsifiable legal errors, making the question conceptually clean.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "categorically unavailable... because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes legislative preemption" — wrong because mere scheduling does not categorically preempt medical necessity; *Oakland Cannabis* requires a specific legislative determination weighing the medical harms (e.g., placing a drug in Schedule I).
- (b): "viable under both standards" — wrong because the common law "clean hands" rule categorically bars the defense when the defendant creates the emergency.
- (d): "categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) only strips the defense for crimes where recklessness/negligence suffices for culpability; it remains viable for intentional/knowing crimes.
- (e): "strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because common law necessity compares the magnitude of competing harms and does not strictly require the threat to be death or SBI (unlike duress).

Recommended fix: In option (b), remove the extraneous fact about the ER bed to prevent students from eliminating the distractor on fact-pattern mismatch rather than legal doctrine. Change to: "The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break objectively outweighs the property damage and unauthorized possession."
-->

## Q12

### Grounding

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Duress (Threat Threshold and Target Scope), Ch 21, Refinements `duress-threat-threshold` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

The distractors cleverly attempt to import those exact timing and escape concepts into the threshold element, perfectly testing whether students understand the boundaries and discrete components of the defense under both standards.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option accurately summarizes the standard definitions of duress. Under common law, the threat of a beating requiring "surgical repair" meets the threshold of "serious bodily injury," and a mother qualifies as a "close relation." Under MPC § 2.09, the standard is even lower ("unlawful force") and the target scope is broader ("any person"), making the defense applicable under both standards.

(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that traditional common law was extremely narrow to prevent fraudulent defenses and collusion. While modern courts have expanded the scope, some older or stricter jurisdictions historically limited the defense to threats against the defendant's own life or limb. This argument posits that the common law target scope remains strictly personal, excluding even close family members like a mother.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the five-day gap between the threat (March 10) and the crime (March 15). Although the MPC text does not use the word "imminent," a student might argue that the "person of reasonable firmness" standard effectively incorporates an imminence requirement. If the threat is for future harm, a person of reasonable firmness would seek police protection rather than committing the crime, thus the MPC functionally retains the common law imminence threshold.

(d) Argument-for: This argument hinges on a strict interpretation of "serious bodily injury." Under a high-threshold common law standard, only threats of death or permanent, life-threatening disfigurement count. One could argue that a "week in the hospital" for a beating is a significant but non-deadly battery that falls short of the "deadly force" or "grievous harm" required by the most rigorous common law formulations, whereas the MPC's "unlawful force" is much easier to satisfy.

(e) Argument-for: This option emphasizes the affirmative duty to avoid the crime when possible. If the defendant had five days to contact authorities, a court might rule that the threat is insufficient as a matter of law because the "reasonable firmness" test (MPC) and the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" test (CL) both require the absence of a safe legal alternative. Failing to report the threat when there is ample time renders the defense unavailable.

Head-to-head: (a) is the strongest answer because it accurately identifies the core expansions and definitions of both the common law and the MPC. (b) is weak because the common law has long recognized "close relatives" in the target scope. (c) is legally incorrect because MPC § 2.09 explicitly abandoned the "imminence" requirement that existed at common law. (d) is incorrect because "serious bodily injury" is a well-established alternative to "deadly force" at common law. (e) is incorrect because it mislabels the "reasonable firmness" test as a common-law standard and wrongly treats a factual question of "opportunity to escape" as an automatic threshold bar for the "threat" element itself.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally." — wrong because common law target scope extends to close relations (parents, spouses, children).
- (c): "Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 purposefully omits imminence, replacing it with the "person of reasonable firmness" test.
- (d): "common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" — wrong because threats of serious bodily injury (not just death) satisfy the common law threshold.
- (e): "fails under both standards because... 'reasonable firmness' test" — wrong because "reasonable firmness" is an MPC-specific term; the common law uses an objective standard focused on "reasonable opportunity to escape."

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code. <!-- correct -->
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation rather than on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, and mere verbal threats of a beating cannot qualify as serious bodily injury.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats do not satisfy the "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person would have reported the threats to police before March 15, rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: This option correctly maps the facts to the doctrinal elements. Under the common law, duress requires a threat of death or serious bodily injury (which a week-long hospital stay clearly satisfies) directed at the defendant or a close relation (which a mother satisfies). The Model Penal Code broadens these elements to "unlawful force" against "another," which is also plainly satisfied here. Thus, the threat threshold and target scope are met under both tests.
(b) Argument-for: A student might recall that common-law duress is highly restrictive compared to the Model Penal Code. By strictly construing the defense, a student could incorrectly deduce that traditional common law requires the threat to be directed solely at the defendant, making the threat against the mother legally immaterial for target scope purposes under the common law.
(c) Argument-for: Imminence is widely known as the hallmark of a common-law duress defense. Because Bentham's threat occurred five days before the robbery, a student could argue it constitutes a threat of future harm. Assuming that the Model Penal Code, while broader in target scope, maintains basic temporal proximity to prevent vigilantism or unnecessary crimes, a student could conclude that the MPC retains strict imminence as part of its threshold requirement.
(d) Argument-for: The common law sets an extremely high bar for duress. A student might interpret the common-law requirement of "death or grievous bodily harm" to practically mean an immediate, lethal threat—like a gun to the head. Under this strict (albeit exaggerated) view, Bentham's verbal threat of a future beating by a third party would fail the common-law threshold entirely, even while passing the MPC's looser "unlawful force" standard.
(e) Argument-for: Both the common law and the Model Penal Code require that the defendant's response meet an objective standard. With a five-day gap between the threat and the crime, an ordinary person of reasonable firmness would have sought police protection. A student could argue that this extended delay and failure to escape defeats the threat threshold as a matter of law, rendering the defense unavailable before it ever reaches a jury.

Head-to-head: Option (a) is correct because it accurately applies the elements of duress for both the common law and MPC to the facts. Every distractor employs an explicit false legal claim to justify its conclusion. Option (b) explicitly denies the "close relation" exception in common law. Option (c) explicitly claims the MPC retains the strict imminence requirement. Option (d) explicitly fabricates an "immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" requirement for the common law. Option (e) conflates the failure to report/escape with the threshold element and falsely classifies the MPC's reasonable firmness test as failing "as a matter of law" in this scenario, rather than being a jury question.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats against "close relations" (such as a mother).
- (c): "the Model Penal Code retains the common-law imminence requirement for the threshold element" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 explicitly drops the strict imminence requirement, replacing it with the broader "reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "the common-law threshold requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force" — wrong because the common law also recognizes threats of serious or grievous bodily injury, not just deadly force.
- (e): "rendering the threat insufficient as a matter of law" — wrong because under the common law, failure to report goes to the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element rather than the threat threshold, and under the MPC, whether a person of reasonable firmness would have been coerced despite the delay is a factual question for the jury.

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume for this question that Kant satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. Evaluate duress as a defense to felony murder under the common law and to murder under the Model Penal Code.

(a) Duress is available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because *United States v. Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death.
(b) Duress is unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any homicide offense.
(c) Although duress is unavailable, Kant may instead assert necessity under Model Penal Code § 3.02's greater-evils balancing, because avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death.
(d) Duress is available for felony murder under the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm; because the armed-car robbery inherently involves firearms, the exception is categorically foreclosed.
(e) Duress is unavailable for felony murder at common law under the traditional rule that extends the murder bar to killings committed during a predicate felony; however, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception makes duress available for felony murder where the defense is available for the predicate felony, and Kant—a non-killer participant whose only link to Bayes's death was driving the van—fits that exception cleanly. Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder. However, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception permits duress for felony murder if the defense excuses the predicate felony and the defendant was a non-killer participant. Given Kant's role as the getaway driver and the stipulation that duress is met for the robbery, Kant fits this exception cleanly. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a homicide exception. (a) is incorrect; *Contento-Pachon* involved drug trafficking, not homicide, and did not disturb the murder bar. (b) incorrectly applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC, which has no such bar. (c) incorrectly invokes necessity; the common law bars necessity for homicide, and avoiding injury is not a greater evil than a human death. (d) invents an ignorance-of-armament requirement; *Reichard* allowed the defense even though the defendant knew her co-felon was armed.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; *People v. Reichard*, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); *McMillan v. State*, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

### Edge Case Audit

**Q13.** Assume for this question that Kant satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. Evaluate duress as a defense to felony murder under the common law and to murder under the Model Penal Code.

(a) Duress is available for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because *United States v. Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death.
(b) Duress is unavailable for felony murder under the common law and for murder under the Model Penal Code, because both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any homicide offense.
(c) Although duress is unavailable, Kant may instead assert necessity under Model Penal Code § 3.02's greater-evils balancing, because avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death.
(d) Duress is available for felony murder under the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm; because the armed-car robbery inherently involves firearms, the exception is categorically foreclosed.
(e) Duress is unavailable for felony murder at common law under the traditional rule that extends the murder bar to killings committed during a predicate felony; however, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception makes duress available for felony murder where the defense is available for the predicate felony, and Kant—a non-killer participant whose only link to Bayes's death was driving the van—fits that exception cleanly. Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception. <!-- correct -->

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder. However, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception permits duress for felony murder if the defense excuses the predicate felony and the defendant was a non-killer participant. Given Kant's role as the getaway driver and the stipulation that duress is met for the robbery, Kant fits this exception cleanly. Under the Model Penal Code, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 lacks a homicide exception. (a) is incorrect; *Contento-Pachon* involved drug trafficking, not homicide, and did not disturb the murder bar. (b) incorrectly applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC, which has no such bar. (c) incorrectly invokes necessity; the common law bars necessity for homicide, and avoiding injury is not a greater evil than a human death. (d) invents an ignorance-of-armament requirement; *Reichard* allowed the defense even though the defendant knew her co-felon was armed.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q13.** Assume for this question that Kant satisfies the elements of duress for the predicate armed robbery, during which his only role was driving the getaway van. Assume further that Bentham (not Kant) fired the fatal shot that killed Bayes. Evaluate duress as a defense to felony murder under the common law and to murder under the Model Penal Code.

(c) Although duress is unavailable, Kant may instead assert necessity under Model Penal Code § 3.02's greater-evils balancing, because avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death.
(d) Duress is available for felony murder under the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm; because the armed-car robbery inherently involves firearms, the exception is categorically foreclosed.
(e) Duress is unavailable for felony murder at common law under the traditional rule that extends the murder bar to killings committed during a predicate felony; however, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception makes duress available for felony murder where the defense is available for the predicate felony, and Kant—a non-killer participant whose only link to Bayes's death was driving the van—fits that exception cleanly. Under Model Penal Code § 2.09, duress is available for murder because § 2.09 contains no homicide exception.

**Answer:** (e)

**Explanation:** (e) is correct. Under traditional common law, the murder bar applies to felony murder. However, the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception permits duress for felony murder if the defense excuses the predicate felony and the defendant was a non-killer participant. (a) is incorrect; *Contento-Pachon* involved drug trafficking, not homicide, and did not disturb the murder bar. (b) incorrectly applies the common-law murder bar to the MPC, which has no such bar. (c) incorrectly invokes necessity; the common law bars necessity for homicide, and avoiding injury is not a greater evil than a human death. (d) invents an ignorance-of-armament requirement; *Reichard* allowed the defense even though the defendant knew her co-felon was armed.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, murder-bar, felony-murder-duress-exception, cross-doctrine], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (CL murder bar; Model Penal Code § 2.09; *People v. Reichard*, 505 Mich. 81 (2020); *McMillan v. State*, 181 Md. App. 298 (2008)).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that the fundamental logic of duress—that a person of reasonable firmness would succumb to an immediate threat of death—should apply regardless of the charge. *United States v. Contento-Pachon* is a seminal case outlining the four-part test for duress (immediate threat, well-grounded fear, no escape). If a defendant can satisfy this high bar, the law should not bifurcate the defense based on the crime charged, as the lack of free will is the same in a drug mule case as it is in a felony murder scenario. This argument views the common law as evolving toward a unified standard where the severity of the threat, rather than the category of the crime, is the primary legal inquiry.

(b) Argument-for: This option rests on the bedrock principle of the "murder bar," which holds that one can never take an innocent life to save one's own. Under this view, both the common law and the MPC share a foundational moral commitment to the sanctity of life that overrides claims of duress in homicide cases. Even though MPC § 2.09 does not explicitly list a homicide exception, a student could argue that many states adopting the MPC specifically re-insert the murder bar via legislative amendment or that courts apply it as a "clear statement" rule from the common law. Under this interpretation, the law categorically refuses to excuse any killing, even those resulting from a predicate felony.

(c) Argument-for: This argument focuses on the MPC’s flexible § 3.02 "choice of evils" provision, which lacks the rigid categorical bars found at common law. Because the threat involves two potential victims (Kant and his mother), a utilitarian balancing test might suggest that preserving two lives (or preventing two serious hospitalizations) is a "greater good" or a "lesser evil" than the death of a single bystander like Bayes. Since the question stipulates that Kant's role was minimal (just driving), the necessity of his participation to save his family members could be presented to a jury as a justified choice under the specific "person of reasonable firmness" standards found in the MPC's broader justifications.

(d) Argument-for: This argument relies on the "clean hands" or "recklessness" limitation inherent in duress. In many jurisdictions, duress is unavailable if the defendant recklessly placed themselves in a situation where they were likely to be subjected to coercion. If Kant knew the robbery was "armed," he was aware of the high probability of lethal force being used. The *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception could be narrowly construed to apply only to those who believe the predicate felony will be non-violent, thereby excluding participants in an armed robbery who should have foreseen the fatal outcome.

(e) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the modern trend in common law jurisdictions like Michigan (*Reichard*) and Maryland (*McMillan*), which distinguishes between the actual killer and a coerced accomplice in felony murder. Since the murder bar's purpose is to prevent the intentional choosing of one life over another, it loses its logical force when applied to a defendant like Kant, who only "chose" to assist in a robbery under duress and did not "choose" the death of Bayes. This is paired correctly with MPC § 2.09, which famously omits the homicide exception that exists at common law, allowing the defense for any crime if the "reasonable firmness" test is met.

Head-to-head: (e) is the only option that accurately reflects both the specific modern common-law exceptions for felony murder and the actual text of MPC § 2.09. (a) overstates the holding of *Contento-Pachon*, which is a drug smuggling case and does not provide a "universal" exception to the murder bar. (b) fails because the MPC notably lacks a categorical murder bar for duress. (c) fails because even under the MPC's liberal necessity standard, a threat of "hospitalization" is legally insufficient to justify the "greater evil" of a human death. (d) fails because the *Reichard*/*McMillan* cases do not impose a requirement of ignorance regarding firearms; rather, they focus on whether the defendant was the "actual killer" or simply a coerced participant in the underlying felony.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*United States v. Contento-Pachon* recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat of death." — wrong because *Contento-Pachon* was a narcotics case that did not address or disturb the common-law murder bar.
- (b): "both frameworks categorically bar duress as a defense to any homicide offense." — wrong because the Model Penal Code § 2.09 does not contain a homicide exception.
- (c): "avoiding Bentham's threat to hospitalize Kant or his mother is a greater evil than Bayes's death." — wrong because under MPC § 3.02, the death of an innocent person is objectively a greater evil than the non-lethal hospitalization of the defendant or their relatives.
- (d): "only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm" — wrong because the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception for felony-murder duress does not require ignorance of the co-felon's armament.

Recommended fix: none
-->

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might choose (a) by recalling that *United States v. Contento-Pachon* stands as a pivotal case establishing the strict elements of duress (immediate threat of death or serious bodily injury, well-grounded fear). Conflating general elements with specific homicide exceptions, a student might argue that *Contento-Pachon* represents a modern trend allowing duress whenever the threat is severe enough. Since the MPC allows duress for murder, the student might assume this "universal exception" applies across the board under common law as well.
(b) Argument-for: A student could confidently select (b) based on the traditional common-law maxim that "duress is never a defense to murder." Given the gravity of taking an innocent life, many jurisdictions rigidly enforce the murder bar across all forms of homicide, including felony murder. By assuming the Model Penal Code adopted the same strict moral baseline as the common law, the student could logically conclude that both frameworks categorically prohibit duress as an excuse for homicide.
(c) Argument-for: A student drawn to (c) might recognize that Model Penal Code § 3.02 abandons the common-law rule categorically barring necessity for homicide. Because the MPC theoretically permits greater-evils balancing even when a life is taken (such as in a trolley-problem scenario), the student might assume that any severe threat could be weighed by a jury. If the threat to Kant's mother was severe, a student might reason that avoiding it could be deemed a "greater evil" under a broad interpretation of the MPC, especially since Kant was only the getaway driver.
(d) Argument-for: Option (d) appeals to students who remember the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception but believe it applies extremely narrowly. The rationale for allowing duress in felony murder is that the defendant lacked homicidal intent or extreme recklessness. A student could infer that knowing a co-felon is armed demonstrates reckless indifference to human life, thereby destroying the rationale for the exception. Thus, they could argue the defense is categorically foreclosed unless the defendant proves ignorance of the firearm.
(e) Argument-for: Option (e) accurately synthesizes the common law and Model Penal Code rules for duress in homicide. Under traditional common law, the duress murder bar extends to felony murder, but the *Reichard*/*McMillan* line of cases carves out an exception for non-killer participants whose predicate felony is excused by duress. Kant fits this perfectly as a mere getaway driver. Additionally, the option correctly states that Model Penal Code § 2.09 deliberately omitted the common-law homicide exception, making duress generally available for murder.

Option (e) correctly applies both the common-law *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception for non-killer participants in felony murder and the MPC's explicit allowance of duress for homicide. Option (a) invents a universal common-law exception derived from *Contento-Pachon*, a case involving drug smuggling rather than homicide. Option (b) falsely asserts the MPC categorically bars duress for homicide, directly contravening § 2.09. Option (c) relies on an objectively false application of MPC necessity; preventing hospitalization is categorically never recognized as a greater evil than a human death. Option (d) fabricates a strict ignorance-of-firearms requirement for the *Reichard* exception. All distractors fail due to clear, explicitly falsifiable claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "recognized a universal common-law duress exception whenever the defendant faces an immediate threat" — wrong because *Contento-Pachon* involved drug offenses, not homicide, and created no universal duress exception for murder.

- (d): "only if Kant can affirmatively prove he was unaware that any crew member carried a firearm" — wrong because the *Reichard*/*McMillan* exception does not require the non-killer participant to be ignorant of weapons.

## Q14

### Grounding

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — objective reasonableness, Ch 22, Refinement `objective-reasonableness-standard` and `situational-incorporation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

(c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Although the Court of Appeals in *Goetz* eventually rejected the Appellate Division's purely subjective test, the core of the defense's argument—and the initial lower court ruling—was that a defendant's actions must be judged from their own perspective to be fair. An advocate could argue that by incorporating the defendant's "situation" and "prior experiences," *Goetz* effectively allows a jury to decide if the act was "reasonable to him." This ensures that the jury accounts for the specific physical and mental state of the defendant at the time of the shooting.

(b) Argument-for: Under *People v. Goetz*, the New York standard for reasonableness is an objective one that nonetheless accounts for the defendant's specific "circumstances" and "situation." This includes any prior knowledge the defendant had of the attacker, such as Bentham's specific threat to hospitalize Kant. Given the immediate physical threat of a leveled firearm and the lack of accessible cover or a nearby exit, a reasonable person possessing Kant's knowledge would logically conclude that deadly force was necessary to prevent his own death or serious injury.

(c) Argument-for: One could argue that to remain truly "objective," the *Goetz* standard must look at the immediate physical facts of the encounter—the six-foot distance and the revolver—rather than the history between the parties. Allowing a jury to consider prior threats or reputations for violence risks turning the trial into a character assassination of the victim rather than a clinical evaluation of the immediate threat. From this perspective, the "reasonable person" is an abstract construct that reacts to the barrel of a gun, not to personal grievances or prior non-lethal threats like a "hospitalization" comment.

(d) Argument-for: An advocate might argue that the inclusion of the word "reasonably" in the New York statute is a mere formal hurdle, and that in practice, New York aligns with the Model Penal Code's emphasis on the defendant's internal belief. Since the law requires the jury to step into the defendant's shoes and look through his eyes, the inquiry is fundamentally subjective. If Kant truly believed he was in danger of death, his belief should satisfy the standard regardless of how an external "reasonable person" might feel.

(e) Argument-for: In many areas of criminal law, such as duress, the "person of reasonable firmness" is the standard for evaluating how much pressure a defendant is expected to withstand. A student might argue that self-defense is essentially a subset of this logic: would a person of reasonable firmness, facing a loaded gun and a prior threat of violence, feel compelled to fire? This provides a clear, high-threshold benchmark that matches the intensity of a lethal encounter like the one between Bentham and Kant.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the strongest because it perfectly captures the "hybrid" nature of the *Goetz* standard—retaining the objective "reasonable person" while populating that person with the defendant's specific situational knowledge. Options (a), (c), and (d) each take one side of the subjective/objective divide that *Goetz* explicitly attempted to bridge, making them legally inaccurate. Option (e) is a "category error" distractor, importing the standard for duress into a self-defense analysis.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because *Goetz* expressly rejected this formulation in favor of an objective standard.
- (c): "*Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the "situation" include the defendant's relevant prior experiences.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because *Goetz* specifically noted that the NY legislature rejected the MPC approach by requiring that the defendant "reasonably" believe force is necessary.
- (e): "controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist firing" — wrong because "reasonable firmness" is the standard for duress under MPC § 2.09, not self-defense under NYPL § 35.15.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q14.** During the warehouse encounter, Bentham leveled his revolver at Kant's chest from six feet away, with the nearest exit fifteen feet behind Kant without cover. Prior to the robbery, Bentham had explicitly threatened to have Kant put in the hospital for a week. Assume that, whether or not Kant was the initial aggressor, his killing of Bentham proceeded to a jury on self-defense. Under New York's reasonableness standard as articulated in *People v. Goetz*, evaluate whether Kant's belief that deadly force was immediately necessary was reasonable.

(a) Kant's belief was reasonable because *Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation, which asks only whether the defendant himself regarded his response as warranted.

(b) A reasonable person in Kant's situation — facing Bentham leveling a revolver at his chest from six feet, with the only exit fifteen feet away without cover, and with knowledge of Bentham's explicit threat of hospitalizing violence — could reasonably believe deadly force was immediately necessary; *Goetz* permits the factfinder to incorporate the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker in the objective inquiry. <!-- correct -->

(c) Kant's belief was unreasonable because the *Goetz* objective standard excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker or the attacker's reputation for violence; those are subjective elements that would convert the reasonable-person test into a defendant-specific inquiry.

(d) Kant's belief was reasonable because New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation, which asks only whether the defendant believed deadly force was necessary, without requiring objective reasonableness.

(e) Kant's belief was reasonable because the controlling inquiry is whether a "person of reasonable firmness" would have been unable to resist firing, and Bentham's leveled revolver and explicit threat plainly overcome that threshold.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly applies *Goetz*, which held that reasonableness incorporates the defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and specific knowledge of the attacker — not a purely external, context-stripped inquiry. The leveled revolver from six feet, the absence of any safe exit, and Bentham's prior explicit threat of hospitalizing violence all inform what a reasonable person in Kant's situation would have perceived. (a) falsely attributes the "reasonable to him" formulation to *Goetz*; the Court of Appeals expressly rejected that Appellate Division reading and retained an objective element. (c) falsely excludes prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker; *Goetz* expressly included them. (d) falsely states that NY follows the Model Penal Code; *Goetz* noted that the legislature "crucially inserted the word 'reasonably' before 'believes,'" rejecting the Model Penal Code's purely subjective approach. (e) imports the Model Penal Code duress standard ("person of reasonable firmness" in § 2.09) into a self-defense question; self-defense asks about the reasonableness of the defensive belief, not about firmness in resisting coercion.

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [self-defense, objective-reasonableness, situational-incorporation, Goetz], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part II (*People v. Goetz*, 68 N.Y.2d 96 (1986): objective standard incorporates defendant's "situation," including prior experiences and knowledge of attacker; rejection of purely subjective Model Penal Code-style test).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that *Goetz* emphasized evaluating the circumstances from the defendant's perspective. The Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" formulation captured the reality that the factfinder must step into the defendant's shoes. Because the Court of Appeals required the jury to consider the defendant's specific background, physical attributes, and prior experiences, a student might conclude that the highest court essentially adopted the "reasonable to him" standard in substance, even if it refined the nomenclature.
(b) Argument-for: A student could point out that *Goetz* famously established a hybrid objective-subjective standard for self-defense in New York. The Court held that while the belief must be objectively reasonable, the "reasonable person" must be placed in the defendant's exact "situation." This situation explicitly includes the physical realities of the encounter (a leveled gun, no cover) as well as the defendant's specific knowledge of the attacker's prior threats. Thus, a reasonable person in Kant's exact situation could find deadly force immediately necessary.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue that *Goetz* explicitly rejected the Model Penal Code's subjective approach in favor of retaining an objective standard. To maintain a true objective standard, the inquiry must be cleansed of the defendant's subjective biases, fears, and idiosyncratic prior experiences. If the jury considers the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker, it risks converting the test back into the rejected subjective "reasonable to him" standard. Therefore, true objective reasonableness must exclude these subjective historical factors.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that the New York penal code revisions in the 1960s were heavily influenced by the Model Penal Code. The MPC deliberately abandoned the strict objective standard for self-defense, requiring only that the actor genuinely believe the force was necessary. A student could mistakenly believe that New York adopted this MPC provision wholesale, meaning Kant's honest, subjective belief is enough to satisfy the statute without an objective reasonableness requirement.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that when an actor is facing an immediate, terrifying threat like a leveled revolver, the situation triggers the "person of reasonable firmness" standard. This standard, widely used in necessity and duress contexts to evaluate objectively reasonable responses to overwhelming pressure, perfectly captures the terror of a gun pointed at one's chest. Thus, the controlling objective test is whether a reasonably firm person would succumb to the pressure and fire.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer, as it accurately applies the *Goetz* standard, which incorporates the defendant's "situation" (including prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker) into the objective reasonable person test. Option (a) fails because it explicitly states *Goetz* "adopted" the Appellate Division's standard, a historically and legally false claim since the Court of Appeals reversed the Appellate Division specifically to reject that formulation. Option (c) fails because it categorically asserts the standard "excludes any reference" to prior experiences, directly contradicting *Goetz*'s holding that the defendant's situation encompasses such experiences. Option (d) fails because it falsely claims New York adopted the "purely subjective formulation" of the MPC without requiring objective reasonableness, ignoring the legislature's addition of the word "reasonably." Option (e) fails because it incorrectly identifies the "person of reasonable firmness" test—which belongs to duress—as the controlling inquiry for self-defense. All distractors feature clear, falsifiable legal errors.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "*Goetz* adopted the Appellate Division's 'reasonable to him' formulation" — wrong because the Court of Appeals expressly rejected the Appellate Division's "reasonable to him" standard to preserve an objective requirement.
- (c): "excludes any reference to the defendant's prior experiences with the attacker" — wrong because *Goetz* explicitly held that the objective standard must incorporate the physical circumstances and the defendant's prior experiences and knowledge of the attacker.
- (d): "New York followed the Model Penal Code's purely subjective formulation" — wrong because the New York legislature deliberately added the word "reasonably" to the MPC's language, requiring objective reasonableness.
- (e): "the controlling inquiry is whether a 'person of reasonable firmness' would have been unable to resist" — wrong because this formulation states the objective standard for the excuse of duress (MPC § 2.09), not the justification of self-defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q15

### Grounding

**Q15.** Based on the standoff at the warehouse, assume a jury finds Bentham posed an imminent threat of deadly force when he leveled his revolver at Kant from six feet away, with Kant fifteen feet from the nearest exit without cover. Further assume Kant's receipt of stolen proceeds legally constituted "criminal activity" despite any potential defenses. Evaluate how the duty-to-retreat analysis would proceed across three jurisdictions: New York, Florida, and California.

(a) Kant is entitled to Stand Your Ground immunity in Florida regardless of the ongoing proceeds transfer, because the statute's "engaged in criminal activity" limitation applies only to activity unrelated to the immediate confrontation; Bentham's aggression severs any connection to the predicate robbery.

(b) The New York duty to retreat is not satisfied unless a reasonably prudent person would have retreated using any available escape route, regardless of safety; the fifteen-foot distance to the exit establishes an available route as a matter of law.

(c) In California, Kant has a duty to retreat because California applies the traditional retreat rule to all non-dwelling confrontations, and the warehouse is a commercial space.

(d) In New York, the duty to retreat applies only if retreat is available "with complete personal safety," and a fifteen-foot dash without cover in front of a leveled revolver likely does not qualify as a safe retreat; in Florida, Kant cannot invoke stand-your-ground immunity because the statute bars immunity where the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity"; in California, there is no duty to retreat. <!-- correct -->

(e) The castle doctrine applies to the warehouse in all three jurisdictions because Kant was a business invitee, and all three recognize a workplace extension of the castle doctrine.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly traces each jurisdiction. NY requires retreat only if available "with complete personal safety"; a fifteen-foot traverse of open floor against a leveled revolver from six feet likely fails that standard. Florida's stand-your-ground statute excludes immunity when the defendant is "engaged in criminal activity" (Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2)), which the prompt stipulates applies here. CA imposes no duty to retreat by case law. (a) fabricates a "severance" doctrine; FL's statutory limitation is categorical. (b) misstates the NY standard; NY requires retreat only with "complete personal safety." (c) misstates CA law; CA imposes no duty to retreat in any setting. (e) fabricates a universal workplace castle-doctrine rule; none of the three treat a warehouse where the defendant is a criminal participant as a "castle."

**Tags:** chapters: [22], topics: [duty-to-retreat, stand-your-ground, castle-doctrine, jurisdictional-variation], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 22, Part I table and Part IV (NY "complete safety" retreat rule; Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) SYG "criminal activity" bar; CA no-duty-to-retreat rule; castle-doctrine scope).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duty-to-retreat/stand-your-ground, Ch 22, Refinement `duty-to-retreat-general`, `stand-your-ground-statute`, and `castle-doctrine-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

<!-- audit: SHOULD FIX
Check 1: Fails. Option (d) incorrectly states that Florida law "bars immunity where the defendant is 'engaged in criminal activity'." Fla. Stat. § 776.012(2) only revokes the *right to stand your ground* (i.e., it reimposes the duty to retreat) for those engaged in criminal activity; it does not categorically bar self-defense justification or the resulting § 776.032 immunity. Because Kant cannot safely retreat (leveled revolver vs. 15 feet of open ground), a prepared student would correctly note that he is still justified under Florida law and still entitled to immunity. 
Check 2: Pass. No other options present a credible distractor, but (d) itself contains a misstatement of law.
Check 3: Fails. The explanation replicates the error from option (d) by stating the statute "excludes immunity."
Check 4: Pass. Facts are sufficient.
Check 5: Pass. Jurisdictional differences are squarely addressed.
Check 6: Pass. No excluded topics present.
Check 7: Pass. Doctrine matches Chapter 22 focus tags.

Recommended fix: 
Change the Florida portion of Option (d) to: "...in Florida, Kant loses the statutory right to stand his ground because the statute excludes those 'engaged in criminal activity,' meaning he reverts to a duty to retreat;"

In the explanation, change: "...Florida's stand-your-ground statute excludes immunity when the defendant is 'engaged in criminal activity'..."
To: "...Florida's stand-your-ground statute revokes the right to stand one's ground (reimposing a duty to retreat) when the defendant is 'engaged in criminal activity'..."
